<meta name="citation_title" content="Draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor (Arachis duranensis) provides insights into geocarpy, oil biosynthesis, and allergens" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Peanut or groundnut (&lt;i&gt;Arachis hypogaea&lt;/i&gt; L.), a legume of South American origin, has high seed oil content (45–56%) and is a staple crop in semiarid tropical and subtropical regions, partially because of drought tolerance conferred by its geocarpic reproductive strategy. We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, &lt;i&gt;Arachis duranensis&lt;/i&gt;, and 50,324 protein-coding gene models. Patterns of gene duplication suggest the peanut lineage has been affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic &lt;i&gt;Arachis&lt;/i&gt; tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion in only three seed-to-seed generations since their formation by human hands, indicating that this process begins virtually immediately following polyploid formation. Expansion of some specific gene families suggests roles in the unusual subterranean fructification of &lt;i&gt;Arachis&lt;/i&gt;. For example, the S1Fa-like transcription factor family has 126 &lt;i&gt;Arachis&lt;/i&gt; members, in contrast to no more than five members in other examined plant species, and is more highly expressed in roots and etiolated seedlings than green leaves. The &lt;i&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/i&gt; genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants, informing peanut genetic improvement and aiding deeper sequencing of &lt;i&gt;Arachis&lt;/i&gt; diversity.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, &lt;i&gt;Arachis duranensis&lt;/i&gt;, providing details on total genes present in the genome. Genome analysis suggests that the peanut lineage was affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic &lt;i&gt;Arachis&lt;/i&gt; tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion since their formation by human hands. The &lt;i&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/i&gt; genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants. This study also provides millions of structural variations that can be used as genetic markers for the development of improved peanut varieties through genomics-assisted breeding.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor (Arachis duranensis) provides insights into geocarpy, oil biosynthesis, and allergens" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/highwire/pnas/113/24.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, Arachis duranensis , providing details on total genes present in the genome. Genome analysis suggests that the peanut lineage was affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic Arachis tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion since their formation by human hands. The A. duranensis genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants. This study also provides millions of structural variations that can be used as genetic markers for the development of improved peanut varieties through genomics-assisted breeding." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, Arachis duranensis , providing details on total genes present in the genome. Genome analysis suggests that the peanut lineage was affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic Arachis tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion since their formation by human hands. The A. duranensis genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants. This study also provides millions of structural variations that can be used as genetic markers for the development of improved peanut varieties through genomics-assisted breeding." />

<li class="author-site-search-link"><a href="/search/author1%3AShanlin%2BYu%2B" rel="nofollow" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Search for this author on this site</a></li>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-6">We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, <em>Arachis duranensis</em>, providing details on total genes present in the genome. Genome analysis suggests that the peanut lineage was affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic <em>Arachis</em> tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion since their formation by human hands. The <em>A. duranensis</em> genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants. This study also provides millions of structural variations that can be used as genetic markers for the development of improved peanut varieties through genomics-assisted breeding.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-7">Peanut or groundnut (<em>Arachis hypogaea</em> L.), a legume of South American origin, has high seed oil content (45–56%) and is a staple crop in semiarid tropical and subtropical regions, partially because of drought tolerance conferred by its geocarpic reproductive strategy. We present a draft genome of the peanut A-genome progenitor, <em>Arachis duranensis</em>, and 50,324 protein-coding gene models. Patterns of gene duplication suggest the peanut lineage has been affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots. Resequencing of synthetic <em>Arachis</em> tetraploids reveals extensive gene conversion in only three seed-to-seed generations since their formation by human hands, indicating that this process begins virtually immediately following polyploid formation. Expansion of some specific gene families suggests roles in the unusual subterranean fructification of <em>Arachis</em>. For example, the S1Fa-like transcription factor family has 126 <em>Arachis</em> members, in contrast to no more than five members in other examined plant species, and is more highly expressed in roots and etiolated seedlings than green leaves. The <em>A. duranensis</em> genome provides a major source of candidate genes for fructification, oil biosynthesis, and allergens, expanding knowledge of understudied areas of plant biology and human health impacts of plants, informing peanut genetic improvement and aiding deeper sequencing of <em>Arachis</em> diversity.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" href="/keyword/arachis-duranensis" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-arachis-duranensis" rel="nofollow"><em xmlns:default="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml">Arachis duranensis</em></a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/genome-sequence" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-genome-sequence" rel="nofollow">genome sequence</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/gene-models" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-gene-models" rel="nofollow">gene models</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/polyploidizations" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-polyploidizations" rel="nofollow">polyploidizations</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/gene-duplication" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-gene-duplication" rel="nofollow">gene duplication</a></li></ul><p id="p-8" class="flushleft">Peanut (<em>Arachis hypogaea</em> L.) is one of the most economically important crops as an important source of edible oil and protein. With yields averaging 2,898 pounds per acre in the United States in recent years, and with seed comprised of a remarkable 45∼56% oil, peanut typically produces more than twice the amount of oil per unit area than soybean (1,059 vs. 446 L/ha). Rudolf Diesel’s revolutionary engine showcased to the world in Paris in 1900 ran on peanut oil.</p><p id="p-9">Rich in oleic and linoleic acids, peanut oil is associated with several human health benefits. Peanut is a good source of protein (24% by weight), resveratrol implicated in improved cardiovascular health, fiber that reduces the risk of certain cancers and controls blood sugar levels, folic acid, which helps prevent neural tube defects, and contains nearly half of the 13 essential vitamins and 35% of the essential minerals. A hindrance to realizing these benefits is the prevalence of peanut allergy, for example affecting 0.8% of children and 0.6% of adults in the United States, totaling more than 1 million Americans (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>).</p><p id="p-10">The genus <em>Arachis</em> is distinguished from most Fabaceae taxa in that all members have geocarpic reproductive habit, with “aerial flower, subterranean fruit” (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). Following fertilization, the peanut gynophore elongates to form a special geotropic “peg” (<a id="xref-ref-2-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>) harboring the embryo, which continues to grow and push the developing pod into the soil. Pod formation and embryo differentiation occur and a seed is produced underground (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>). Thought to be an adaptation to particularly harsh environments, geocarpy has been reported in only 24 families and 57 genera of flowering plants (<a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>).</p><p id="p-11"><em>Arachis</em> originated in South America and its ∼80 species have been divided taxonomically into nine sections (<a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-5">5</a>). <em>A. hypogaea</em>, cultivated peanut or groundnut, was domesticated (<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>) and is now grown on 25 million ha between latitudes 40° N and 40° S, with global production of 36 million tons (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org/" class="in-nw">faostat.fao.org</a>). Peanut is allotetraploid (2n = 4x = 40), with an AABB genomic constitution and genome size of 2,800 Mb (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>); its suspected recent origin is thought to account for the paucity of genetic diversity among naturally occurring peanut genotypes. However, the genetic vulnerability of natural tetraploids is in contrast to rich diversity among diploid <em>Arachis</em>.</p><p id="p-12">To gain insight into peanut evolution and opportunities for mitigating its genetic vulnerability, we sequenced the suspected peanut A-genome progenitor, <em>Arachis duranensis</em>, as well as four synthetic tetraploids and their six diploid parents [two A-genome and four B-genome, including the suspected B-genome progenitor, <em>Arachis ipaensis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>, <a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>)]. Complementing an independent effort to sequence tetraploid peanut, this approach provides new insight into <em>Arachis</em> biology and evolution and into the nature and rate of genomic change following polyploid formation.</p><div class="section results-discussion" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results and Discussion</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Assembly and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-13">The genome of <em>A. duranensis</em> (accession <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=PI475845&amp;atom=%2Fpnas%2F113%2F24%2F6785.atom">PI475845</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>) was sequenced using a shotgun strategy. About 229.94 Gb of raw data from a Hiseq2500 (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>SI Text</em> and Table S1</a>) yielded an optimized assembly of 1.05 Gb with an N50 contig length of 29.6 kb and N50 scaffold length of 649.8 kb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>SI Text</em>, Fig. S2 and Tables S2 and S3</a>). PCR amplification of randomly selected regions, sequence-depth distribution, and expressed sequence tag validation (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S3–S6 and Tables S4–S6</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd01.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>) indicated the high quality of the assembled genome. <em>K</em>-mer analysis indicated <em>A. duranensis</em> genome size of 1.38 Gb (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S7</a>), consistent with prior data (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). The average heterozygosity rate was estimated to be three SNPs per kilobase (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7</a>). The average GC content is 31.79% (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>) and its distribution is similar to other legumes, although different from other oilseeds and other plants (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S8 and S9</a>).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/519356/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/519356/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-14" class="first-child">Summary of genome assembly and genome annotation of diploid A-genome progenitor</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-15">We predicted 50,324 protein-coding gene models (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), with 99% (50,281) supported by transcriptome sequences (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). Compared with the gene sets of legumes, oilseeds, and other plant species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S8</a>), <em>A. duranensis</em> genes showed highest similarity to legumes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S9</a>), with gene number comparable to <em>Medicago truncatula</em> (50,894), lower than soybean (tetraploid <em>Glycine max</em>, 56,044), and higher than other legumes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S10</a>). Of the 50,324 gene models, ∼90% matched entries in publically available databases (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S10 and Table S11</a>). Approximately 10.9% (5,494) of gene models with no homology to known proteins were supported by transcriptome data and may be peanut-specific. Comparison of gene characteristics indicated that distribution of <em>A. duranensis</em> gene features were similar to legumes, but obviously different from distantly related plant species (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S11</a>). <em>A. duranensis</em> has an average of one gene per 21.4 kb (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-1-4" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>), half the gene density of <em>Lotus</em> (10.2 kb) (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>) and one-fifth that of <em>Arabidopsis</em> (4.5 kb) (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). The average gene length of 3,057 bp, protein length of 368 aa, coding sequence length of 312 bp with 3.37 exons, and intron length of 709 bp were relatively long among plant species (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-5" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S10</a>). Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis revealed genes overrepresented in processes such as gravitropism under reaction to stimulus (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S12–S14</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S2–S4</a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="A. duranensis genome overview. From the outer edge inward, circles represent the 50 largest DNA sequence scaffolds (green), the genes on each scaffold (purple), SNP density at 100-kb intervals (blue), repeat density at 100 kb (green), transposable element density at 100 kb (yellow), and the fold-change values of transcripts (red). Links in the core connect duplicated sets of genes (E-value threshold of " class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-821671376" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;&lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; genome overview. From the outer edge inward, circles represent the 50 largest DNA sequence scaffolds (green), the genes on each scaffold (purple), SNP density at 100-kb intervals (blue), repeat density at 100 kb (green), transposable element density at 100 kb (yellow), and the fold-change values of transcripts (red). Links in the core connect duplicated sets of genes (E-value threshold of &lt;1e-10 and 85% similarity).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/519266" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child"><em>A. duranensis</em> genome overview. From the outer edge inward, circles represent the 50 largest DNA sequence scaffolds (green), the genes on each scaffold (purple), SNP density at 100-kb intervals (blue), repeat density at 100 kb (green), transposable element density at 100 kb (yellow), and the fold-change values of transcripts (red). Links in the core connect duplicated sets of genes (E-value threshold of &lt;1e-10 and 85% similarity).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Content and Repeated Sequence Annotation.</h3><p id="p-17">In 16 diverse plant species with 54,384 gene families identified using OrthoMCL (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S15–S20 and Tables S12 and S13</a>), 1,423 families were specific to <em>A. duranensis</em>. Comparison with soybean and <em>Medicago</em> suggested that <em>A. duranensis</em> family-size variations were different from those of other legumes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S13</a>). A total of 9,968 single-copy orthologs and 16,472 unique paralogs were identified in <em>A. duranensis</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S21 and Table S14</a>).</p><p id="p-18">We identified 5,251 putative <em>A. duranensis</em> transcription factor (TF) genes in 57 families, 10.4% of the predicted <em>A. duranensis</em> genes, slightly higher than soybean, and much higher than most plant species analyzed (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). Although distributed among families in a manner generally similar to soybean (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S22</a>), a few TF families were dominant in <em>A. duranensis</em>, such as B3, E2F/DP, FAR1, GeBP, HSF, NAC, S1Fa-like, and STAT (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). Families such as ARR-B, CAMTA, DBB, MIKC, and NF-YA, were sparser in <em>A. duranensis</em> than in most plants. Expansion and contraction of TF families may reflect regulatory differences in biological functions of <em>A. duranensis</em>.</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Comparative genomic and evolutionary analysis. (A) Scatter plot of percentage of A. duranensis transcription factors in relation to soybean, Medicago, Lotus, chickpea, and pigeonpea. (B) Syntenic relationship between A. duranensis scaffolds and soybean chromosomes. (C) Phylogenetic tree for 16 plant species based on single copy orthologous genes. (D) Distribution of synonymous nucleotide substitutions (Ks) for A. duranensis, soybean, Medicago, and grape. (E) Allelic changes between the A and B subgenomes of synthetic tetraploid peanut lines, ISATGR 184 (Upper) and ISATGR 1212 (Lower)." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-821671376" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Comparative genomic and evolutionary analysis. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Scatter plot of percentage of &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; transcription factors in relation to soybean, &lt;em&gt;Medicago&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Lotus&lt;/em&gt;, chickpea, and pigeonpea. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Syntenic relationship between &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; scaffolds and soybean chromosomes. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Phylogenetic tree for 16 plant species based on single copy orthologous genes. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Distribution of synonymous nucleotide substitutions (&lt;em&gt;K&lt;/em&gt;s) for &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt;, soybean, &lt;em&gt;Medicago&lt;/em&gt;, and grape. (&lt;em&gt;E&lt;/em&gt;) Allelic changes between the A and B subgenomes of synthetic tetraploid peanut lines, ISATGR 184 (&lt;em&gt;Upper&lt;/em&gt;) and ISATGR 1212 (&lt;em&gt;Lower&lt;/em&gt;).&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="301"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="301"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/519810" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-19" class="first-child">Comparative genomic and evolutionary analysis. (<em>A</em>) Scatter plot of percentage of <em>A. duranensis</em> transcription factors in relation to soybean, <em>Medicago</em>, <em>Lotus</em>, chickpea, and pigeonpea. (<em>B</em>) Syntenic relationship between <em>A. duranensis</em> scaffolds and soybean chromosomes. (<em>C</em>) Phylogenetic tree for 16 plant species based on single copy orthologous genes. (<em>D</em>) Distribution of synonymous nucleotide substitutions (<em>K</em>s) for <em>A. duranensis</em>, soybean, <em>Medicago</em>, and grape. (<em>E</em>) Allelic changes between the A and B subgenomes of synthetic tetraploid peanut lines, ISATGR 184 (<em>Upper</em>) and ISATGR 1212 (<em>Lower</em>).</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-20">We annotated 816 <em>Arachis</em> microRNAs (miRNAs), 913 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 115 ribosomal RNAs (rRNAs), and 202 small nucleolar RNAs (snRNAs) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-6" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S15</a>, and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd06.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S6</a>). A total of 64 target genes were predicted after aligning 15 new miRNAs to our gene models (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S16</a>). These target genes were annotated in diverse GO categories (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S23 and Table S17</a>).</p><p id="p-21">About ∼59.77% of the <em>A. duranensis</em> genome appeared to be transposable elements (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>), with ∼40% long terminal repeat retrotransposons, and with one major burst of amplification ∼2 Mya (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S24</a>). Most <em>A. duranensis</em> transposable element sequences had a &gt;10% divergence rate (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S25</a>). In addition, we identified 105,003 simple sequence repeats (SSRs) in <em>A. duranensis</em>, and resequencing of two other A-genome genotypes and four B-genome genotypes (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S18 and S19</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S7–S10</a>) allowed the discovery of ∼8 million SNPs and other structural variations, useful as molecular markers (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Tables S20 and S21</a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd11.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S11</a>).</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/519762/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/519762/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Organization of repetitive sequences in the diploid A-genome progenitor</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Analysis.</h3><p id="p-24">Synteny analysis based on corresponding genes between genomes revealed high numbers of syntenic blocks between <em>A. duranensis</em> and soybean and common bean, and low numbers with <em>Arabidopsis</em> and rice (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S26</a>, and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd12.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S12</a>). A species tree based on single-copy orthologous genes indicated <em>A. duranensis</em> to be in an individual clade, not including any other legume species and consistent with its phylogenetic placement (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Speciation of Peanut A and B Subgenomes.</h3><p id="p-25">By performing a trio comparison of the synthetic tetraploid ISATGR 184 and its parents, ICG 8123 and ICG 8206, we studied the divergence between the A and B subgenomes. A total of 847,676 high-quality single nucleotide variants were identified between the two parental lines, meaning a mutation rate of ∼4.5 × 10<sup>−4</sup> in each line. Upon mapping the reads of ISATGR 184 onto the reference genome, we identified 748,802 single nucleotide variants sites between the two parental lines.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Recursive Peanut-Specific Genome Duplications.</h3><p id="p-26">Peanut shares a genome duplication with <em>Medicago</em> and soybean. Collinear genes from <em>Medicago</em>, soybean, and grape were used to locate related evolutionary events. The synonymous nucleotide substitution (<em>K</em>s) distribution of peanut homologs shows a prominent peak around <em>K</em>s = 0.5 (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>D</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S27</a>), overlapping the peak of soybean duplicated genes resulting from a pan-legume tetraploidization previously inferred to be ∼60 Mya (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S28</a>). Adding the pan-eudicot γ-hexaploidy (∼130 Mya) and polyploidy-producing tetraploid peanut by joining the <em>Arachis</em> A and B subgenomes, estimated to have diverged 3.5 Mya (<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>), the <em>Arachis</em> lineage has been affected by at least three polyploidizations since the origin of eudicots, with a collective 12× paleo-duplication depth.</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>Gene Conversion has Already Occurred in Synthetic Polyploids.</h3><p id="p-27">Following polyploid formation in plants, extensive and highly structured unidirectional homeologous exchanges between genes from different subgenomes can overwrite one progenitor allele with additional copies of the other, often called gene conversion (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>, <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). Implicated as a possible contributor to the transgressive properties of polyploids relative to their progenitors (<a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), extensive gene conversion has occurred as recently as the past 7,500–12,500 y since formation of the Neolithic species <em>Brassica napus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>).</p><p id="p-28">By performing a three-way comparison of the synthetic tetraploid ISATGR 184 and its progenitor lines, ICG 8123 and ICG 8206, we find evidence of extensive gene conversion between the At and Bt subgenomes in the ∼three seed-to-seed generations since its formation by human hands. The vast majority (∼93%) of alleles have been converted to homozygosity for the A genome allele in ISATGR 184 (<a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>E</em></a>), an asymmetry resembling those found in cotton and canola (<a id="xref-ref-16-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>, <a id="xref-ref-18-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a>). Further analysis with only the singleton genes in the present peanut dataset revealed a similar conclusion (not shown). ISATGR 1212, a reciprocal cross between the same parental lines as ISATGR 184, shared Bt to At bias of conversion (<a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>E</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd13.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S13</a>) but had far fewer converted sites than ISATGR 184 (χ<sup>2</sup> &lt;&lt; 0.001), perhaps indicating a contribution of germ-line types to genomic variation in the offspring.</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>Aerial Flower but Subterranean Fruit.</h3><p id="p-29">A characteristic feature of <em>Arachis</em> is the gynophore (“peg”), a specialized organ that transitions from upward growth habit to downward outgrowth upon fertilization, driving the developing pod into the soil. <em>Arachis</em> pod formation, embryo differentiation, and seed production occur in subterranean darkness (<a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genes, TFs, and pathways related to the biological process &#x201C;Aerial flower, subterranean fruit.&#x201D; (A) Peanut fruit development (2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 9, 12, and 17 indicate days after fertilization, DAF). Upon fertilization the gynophore reverts from upward growth after sensing gravity followed by signal transduction and organ response. Seed development is triggered only if the elongated peg penetrates soil. (B) Comparison of &#x3C9; (Ka/Ks) of gravitropic orthologs in Arabidopsis thaliana-A. duranensis and A. thaliana-G. max for genes involved in photomorphogenesis (blue) or gravitropism (red). Circled genes show evidence of positive selection in both A. duranensis and G. max. (C) Gravitropism phases (gravity sensing, signal transduction and organ response) where three functionally identified genes (ARL2, AUX1, and RGD3) were found positively selected. (D) Numbers of TF family members identified in A. duranensis compared with other plant species (blue indicating low and red indicating high numbers). TFs in brackets have been found in large numbers in A. duranensis." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-821671376" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genes, TFs, and pathways related to the biological process &#x201C;Aerial flower, subterranean fruit.&#x201D; (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Peanut fruit development (2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 9, 12, and 17 indicate days after fertilization, DAF). Upon fertilization the gynophore reverts from upward growth after sensing gravity followed by signal transduction and organ response. Seed development is triggered only if the elongated peg penetrates soil. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Comparison of &#x3C9; (&lt;em&gt;K&lt;/em&gt;&lt;sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;a&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/sub&gt;&lt;em&gt;/K&lt;/em&gt;s) of gravitropic orthologs in &lt;em&gt;Arabidopsis thaliana&lt;/em&gt;-&lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. thaliana&lt;/em&gt;-&lt;em&gt;G. max&lt;/em&gt; for genes involved in photomorphogenesis (blue) or gravitropism (red). Circled genes show evidence of positive selection in both &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;G. max&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Gravitropism phases (gravity sensing, signal transduction and organ response) where three functionally identified genes (&lt;em&gt;ARL2, AUX1&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;RGD3&lt;/em&gt;) were found positively selected. (&lt;em&gt;D&lt;/em&gt;) Numbers of TF family members identified in &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; compared with other plant species (blue indicating low and red indicating high numbers). TFs in brackets have been found in large numbers in &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="218"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F3.medium.gif" width="440" height="218"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/519695" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-30" class="first-child">Genes, TFs, and pathways related to the biological process “Aerial flower, subterranean fruit.” (<em>A</em>) Peanut fruit development (2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 9, 12, and 17 indicate days after fertilization, DAF). Upon fertilization the gynophore reverts from upward growth after sensing gravity followed by signal transduction and organ response. Seed development is triggered only if the elongated peg penetrates soil. (<em>B</em>) Comparison of ω (<em>K</em><sub><em>a</em></sub><em>/K</em>s) of gravitropic orthologs in <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em>-<em>A. duranensis</em> and <em>A. thaliana</em>-<em>G. max</em> for genes involved in photomorphogenesis (blue) or gravitropism (red). Circled genes show evidence of positive selection in both <em>A. duranensis</em> and <em>G. max</em>. (<em>C</em>) Gravitropism phases (gravity sensing, signal transduction and organ response) where three functionally identified genes (<em>ARL2, AUX1</em>, and <em>RGD3</em>) were found positively selected. (<em>D</em>) Numbers of TF family members identified in <em>A. duranensis</em> compared with other plant species (blue indicating low and red indicating high numbers). TFs in brackets have been found in large numbers in <em>A. duranensis</em>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-31">We searched our gene set against genes in the GO category “gravitropism” (GO:0009630) and experimentally identified in <em>Arabidopsis</em> using an E-value cut-off of 1E-10, identifying 151 gravitropism-related genes expressed during pod development. Using the branch-site likelihood ratio test, eight <em>A. duranensis</em> genes showed evidence of positive selection, (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>, <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S29</a>, and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd14.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S14</a>). Three of these genes were identified in <em>Arabidopsis</em> by several lines of experimental evidence (<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a><a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-20"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>) to be involved in signal transduction and organ response (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a>). Further analysis based on previous functional studies (<a id="xref-ref-19-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>, <a id="xref-ref-21-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a><a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-22"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-24"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>) identified 24 <em>A. duranensis</em> genes likely to be gravitropic, including 4 involved in gravity perception, 8 in signal transduction, and 12 in organ response (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd15.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S15</a>).</p><p id="p-32">Fruit development, a genetically controlled process under light conditions, occurs in darkness (subterranean fructification) in <em>Arachis</em>, with peg elongation responding conversely to light/dark conditions (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>). Five TF families related to photomorphogenesis were identified in very large numbers in <em>A. duranensis</em>, namely S1Fa-like, FAR1, HSF, NAC, and STAT (<a id="xref-fig-3-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>D</em></a>). Notably, <em>A. duranensis</em> has 126 S1Fa-like TFs versus no more than 5 in other plants (<a id="xref-fig-3-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>D</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd05.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). S1Fa-like TFs containing a small peptide (70 aa) with a nuclear localization and DNA binding domain were more highly expressed in roots and etiolated seedlings than green leaves (<a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>). Also greatly expanded in <em>A. duranensis</em> is the FAR1 TF family, which plays an important role in modulating phyA-signaling homeostasis in higher plants (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>). Importantly, phyA localized in the cytosol of dark-grown seedlings acts primarily as a far-red sensor, which regulates the transition from skotomorphogenesis to photomorphogenesis (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Furthermore, each phytochrome shows strong dark reversion in some species (<a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). PhyB, exhibiting a fast and strong but incomplete dark conversion in some cases, is the main light receptor responsible for the shade-avoidance response in mature plants (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>) and shows evidence of positive selection in <em>A. duranensis</em> (<a id="xref-fig-3-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>), suggesting a role in skotomorphogenesis.</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Oil Biosynthesis and Allergen-Encoding Genes.</h3><p id="p-33">Comparative genome analysis identified 1,671 <em>A. duranensis</em> genes related to acyl lipid synthesis, comparable to soybean (1,695 genes) and more than the nonoilseed plant <em>Arabidopsis</em> (1,291 genes). Significant enrichment of lipid gene-functional groups were found in both <em>A. duranensis</em> and soybean relative to <em>Arabidopsis</em> (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S22</a>). <em>A. duranensis</em> and soybean had small differences in gene numbers for five categories of lipid synthesis genes (phospholipase, lipid related, fatty acid synthesis in plastids, lipase, and cuticular wax synthesis), whereas the remaining 17 categories were indistinguishable.</p><p id="p-34">Considering the importance of peanut as an oil crop, we manually investigated the annotations of 67 gene models with similarity to known genes involved in fatty acid biosynthesis and triacylglycerol (TAG) assembly, which in peanut correspond mainly to oleic and linoleic acids (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>). Of these 67 gene models, 51 have homologs in the castor genome (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd16.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S16</a>), and 62 were supported by RNA-seq data from peanut seeds during seed filling, maturation, and desiccation (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S30 and Table S23</a>). <em>FAD2</em> encoding δ-12 oleic acid desaturase, the key enzyme controlling the high oleate trait (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>), was expressed highly during seed filling but little during desiccation (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>). Genes encoding key enzymes in the TAG pathway were expressed at diverse levels at different developmental stages (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>). Multiple copies or isoforms of some key genes were detected in the <em>A. duranensis</em> genome like glycerol-3-phosphate acyltransferase and diacylglycerol acyltransferase, which catalyze the first and final steps in the TAG pathway. Information on copy number and expression diversity of these metabolic genes is important for improvement of oil quality parameters in peanut, such as a high oleic to linoleic acid ratio (O/L).</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genes involved in oil biosynthesis and encoding allergens. (A) Expression patterns of genes involved in fatty acid synthesis and TAG assembly in peanut seed. Expression levels were estimated by fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped fragments (FPKM) for each gene obtained by sequencing RNA samples from peanut seeds at six developmental stages. (B) Phylogenetic tree of allergens and their homologs identified in A. hypogaea and A. duranensis and other allergenic crops (wheat and soybean). A total of 69 allergens or homologous genes from 14 families group into 6 major clusters differentiated with colors. Different families having the same colors are more similar to each other than to other families. &#x201C;Dots&#x201D; indicate genes identified in the A. duranensis genome and &#x201C;triangles&#x201D; indicate genes previously identified in A. hypogaea. This figure also shows the distribution of 12 newly identified putative allergen genes from the A. duranensis assembly falling into different clusters and families." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-821671376" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genes involved in oil biosynthesis and encoding allergens. Expression levels were estimated by fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped fragments (FPKM) for each gene obtained by sequencing RNA samples from peanut seeds at six developmental stages. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Phylogenetic tree of allergens and their homologs identified in &lt;em&gt;A. hypogaea&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; and other allergenic crops (wheat and soybean). A total of 69 allergens or homologous genes from 14 families group into 6 major clusters differentiated with colors. Different families having the same colors are more similar to each other than to other families. &#x201C;Dots&#x201D; indicate genes identified in the &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; genome and &#x201C;triangles&#x201D; indicate genes previously identified in &lt;em&gt;A. hypogaea&lt;/em&gt;. This figure also shows the distribution of 12 newly identified putative allergen genes from the &lt;em&gt;A. duranensis&lt;/em&gt; assembly falling into different clusters and families.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="199"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 4." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F4.medium.gif" width="440" height="199"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/113/24/6785/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/519372" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-35" class="first-child">Genes involved in oil biosynthesis and encoding allergens. (<em>A</em>) Expression patterns of genes involved in fatty acid synthesis and TAG assembly in peanut seed. Expression levels were estimated by fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped fragments (FPKM) for each gene obtained by sequencing RNA samples from peanut seeds at six developmental stages. (<em>B</em>) Phylogenetic tree of allergens and their homologs identified in <em>A. hypogaea</em> and <em>A. duranensis</em> and other allergenic crops (wheat and soybean). A total of 69 allergens or homologous genes from 14 families group into 6 major clusters differentiated with colors. Different families having the same colors are more similar to each other than to other families. “Dots” indicate genes identified in the <em>A. duranensis</em> genome and “triangles” indicate genes previously identified in <em>A. hypogaea</em>. This figure also shows the distribution of 12 newly identified putative allergen genes from the <em>A. duranensis</em> assembly falling into different clusters and families.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-36">Peanut allergy, one of most serious life-threatening food sensitivities, is becoming increasingly prevalent, particularly among children. Comparison with known allergenic proteins from peanut and other crops identified 21 candidate allergen-encoding genes in <em>A. duranensis</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>B</em></a> and <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sd17.xlsx" class="in-nw">Dataset S17</a>), of which 9 have already been reported in peanut (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S31</a>), whereas the remaining 12 (<a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S32</a>) are <em>A. duranensis</em> homologs of genes from other crops. Interestingly, we found three putative paralogous genes for <em>Ara h 8</em> in <em>A. duranensis</em>, but none for <em>Ara h 3</em>, which had 16 homologous sequences in peanut (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). Sequence information for additional allergen-encoding genes may nurture studies leading to either genetic or medical approaches to allergy mitigation.</p></div></div><div class="section" id="sec-10"><h2 class="">Concluding Remarks</h2><p id="p-37" class="flushleft">This draft <em>A. duranensis</em> genome provides rich new information about an important branch of the legume clade, building on the genomes of <em>Lotus</em> (<a id="xref-ref-11-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>), <em>Medicago</em> (<a id="xref-ref-14-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>), soybean (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>), pigeonpea (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>), chickpea (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>), and common bean (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>). The <em>Arachis</em> genome sequences presented here provide new insights and new resources for evolution and polyploidization research. The biological interpretation of the <em>A. duranensis</em> genome enhances understanding of the unusual fruit development of peanut. Resequencing 10 additional genotypes including four tetraploids provides a backdrop for reference sequencing of cultivated peanut, as well as molecular markers (SSR or SNP) for investigating genetic diversity and aiding breeding programs.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-11"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><p id="p-38" class="flushleft">The diploid species <em>A. duranensis</em> (AA 2n = 2x = 20) is the most possible progenitor and donor of the A subgenome to cultivated tetraploid (AABB 2n = 4x = 40) peanut (<a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>, <a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>, <a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>, <a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>). Within species <em>A. duranensis</em>, the accession PI 475845 falls into the group that includes 14 of the total 18 accessions (<a id="xref-ref-39-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). Furthermore, South America is the center of origin, the accession <a class="external-ref external-ref-type-gen" href="/lookup/external-ref?link_type=GEN&amp;access_num=PI475845&amp;atom=%2Fpnas%2F113%2F24%2F6785.atom">PI475845</a>  is a collection from Bolivia. Therefore, being a representative accession, we have sequenced the genome of PI 475845 (<em>A. duranensis</em>). See <a href="http://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1600899113/-/DCSupplemental/pnas.1600899113.sapp.pdf" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em></a> for the details of sequencing, assembly, annotation, structural variation, evolutionary, synteny, and transcriptomic analysis.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-39">This project was supported by the National Natural Science Foundation of China (31501246 and 31271767); the Modern Agro-industry Technology Research System (CARS-14); the Science and Technology Planning Project of Guangdong Province (2015B020231006, 2016B020201003, 2012B050700007, and S2013020012647); Pearl River Science and Technology Nova of Guangzhou (2013J2200088), the Southeastern US Region Peanut Research Initiative; the Georgia Peanut Commission; the University System of Georgia Regent’s Professorship funds; and USAID–ICRISAT (International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics) Linkage Grants. This work has also been undertaken as part of the CGIAR (Consultive Group for International Agricultural Research) Research Program on Grain Legumes. ICRISAT is a member of the CGIAR Consortium.</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-3"><p id="p-3"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-3-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>X.     <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/podcast-2021-01-04-220.png?itok=7WJRcWOH" width="218" height="220" alt="White and blue bird" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Hazards of ozone pollution to birds</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description text-small">Amanda Rodewald, Ivan Rudik, and Catherine Kling talk about the hazards of ozone pollution to birds.</div>
    

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__listen"><a href="https://traffic.libsyn.com/secure/pnas-science-sessions-podcast/amandaRodewaldIvanRudikCatherineKlingPodcast.mp3" target="_blank" class="button-alt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Listen</a></div>

  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-odd views-row-last">

  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Transplantation of sperm-producing stem cells</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing can improve the effectiveness of spermatogonial stem cell transplantation in mice and livestock, a study finds.</div>
